Saying Ameen Silently: Ruling and Evidence
Ruling on saying ameen silently in prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on saying 'ameen' silently in prayer and explain the evidence from the hadith, including the implication for the imam and ma'mum.

Core Idea
The imam and ma'mum both say 'ameen' silently; the hadith implies the imam's saying it by commanding the ma'mum to follow.

In this lesson, we examine the ruling on saying 'ameen' after reciting al-Fatihah in prayer. The Hanafi school holds that both the imam and the follower say it silently, based on the well-known hadith from Bukhari and Muslim. We will see how the evidence is derived and why a contrary weak narration is set aside.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is saying 'ameen' after al-Fatihah in prayer. The Hanafi school classifies it as sunnah muakkadah (emphasized sunnah) for the imam, ma'mum, and munfarid.
Sunnah muakkadah means it is regularly practiced and recommended; omission without reason is blameworthy.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary evidence is the hadith: 'When the imam says ameen, then say ameen...' narrated by Bukhari and Muslim.
This hadith is ahad (single-chain) but accepted due to its authenticity and practice.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith commands the ma'mum to say ameen when the imam does so. This implies that the imam also says ameen, because the command to follow presupposes an action to follow.
For the ma'mum, the obligation is explicit; for the imam, it is by implication (dalalah al-isharah).
4
Note the ikhtilaf
There is a weak narration from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad from Abu Hanifa that the imam does not say ameen. This is rejected as it contradicts the stronger hadith and the practice of the Prophet.
The weak narration is considered shadhdh (irregular) and not relied upon.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The preferred (mufta bihi) position is that both the imam and ma'mum say ameen silently, supported by the hadith and the Prophet's practice of lowering his voice. The weak narration is discarded.
The authority is from the zahir al-riwayah (core Hanafi texts).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله ( وأمن الإمام والمأموم سراً) للحديث إذا أمن الإمام فأمنوا فإنه من وافق تأمينه تأمين الملائكة غفر له ما تقدم من ذنبه (۱) رواه الشيخان وهو يفيد تأمينهما لكن في حق الإمام بالإشارة لأنه لم يسق النص له وفي حق المأموم بالعبارة لأنه سيق لأجله، وبهذا يضعف رواية الحسن عن أبي حنيفة أن الإمام لا يؤمن . وروى أبو داود وغيره أنه قال آمين وخفض بها صوته ولو قال المصنف وأمن المصلي أو الجميع كما في الحاوي القدسي لكان أولى، ليشمل المنفرد فإنه يؤمن أيضاً لرواية مسلم إذا قال أحدكم في الصلاة آمين (۲)
His saying: 'And the imam and the ma'mum say 'ameen' silently' due to the hadith: 'When the imam says 'ameen', then say 'ameen', for whoever's 'ameen' coincides with the 'ameen' of the angels, his previous sins will be forgiven.' Narrated by al-Bukhari and Muslim. It indicates that both say 'ameen', but regarding the imam by implication because the text is not explicitly for him, and regarding the ma'mum by explicit wording because the text was stated for his sake. By this, the narration of al-Hasan from Abu Hanifa that the imam does not say 'ameen' is weakened. And Abu Dawud and others narrated that he (the Prophet) said 'ameen' and lowered his voice with it. And if the author had said 'And the worshiper says 'ameen'' or 'all' as in al-Hawi al-Qudsi, it would have been better, to include the one praying alone, for he also says 'ameen' due to the narration of Muslim: 'When one of you says in prayer 'ameen'...'
Source pages p. 547

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on saying 'ameen' after reciting al-Fatihah in prayer. The matn states that the imam and the ma'mum say 'ameen' silently. The commentator provides the hadith evidence from Bukhari and Muslim, which commands the congregation to say 'ameen' when the imam does. The hadith implies that the imam also says 'ameen' because the ma'mum's 'ameen' follows the imam's. This refutes a weak narration from al-Hasan b. Ziyad from Abu Hanifa that the imam does not say 'ameen'. The commentator also notes that the munfarid (one praying alone) should say 'ameen', supported by another hadith. The preferred position is that all say 'ameen' silently, as practiced in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
التأمين (al-ta'min): Saying 'ameen' after al-Fatihah
الإمام (al-imam): The leader of congregational prayer
المأموم (al-ma'mum): The follower in congregational prayer
المنفرد (al-munfarid): One who prays alone

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because the hadith implies it through the command to the ma'mum.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school affirms that both imam and ma'mum say 'ameen' silently after al-Fatihah, based on the hadith and the practice of the Prophet.

Minimum Recitation in Salah: Wajib and Karahah Levels
Explains the minimum wajib recitation and the two karahahs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the minimum wajib recitation in Hanafi salah, identify the two levels of karahah (tahrimiyyah and tanzihiyyah), and reconcile apparent contradictions about leaving karahah.

Core Idea
Three short verses or one long verse are wajib; reciting less triggers karahah tahrimiyyah, but the prayer remains valid.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the author explains the minimum recitation in prayer and clarifies the distinction between two types of karahah. Many students confuse the ruling: they think reciting less invalidates the prayer, or that any dislike is the same. This passage resolves these confusions by showing that the prayer is valid but carries a prohibitive dislike if the wajib is left, and a lighter dislike if the sunnah amount is not reached.

Guided Path
1
Identify the wajib act
Determine what amount of Qur'anic recitation is obligatory (wajib) in salah according to the Hanafi school. The text states: three short verses or one long verse.
A short verse is approximately the length of a verse from Surah al-Kawthar; a long verse is like Ayat al-Kursi.
2
Understand the basis for the wajib amount
Why are three short verses or one long verse considered wajib? Because three short verses equal a surah in inimitability (i'jaz), and a long verse also equals a surah. This analogy (qiyas) establishes the obligation.
The i'jaz (inimitability) is a quality of the Qur'an; the amount that demonstrates i'jaz is taken as the standard.
3
Apply the rule: consequence of falling short
If a person recites less than three short verses or one long verse, he has abandoned the wajib. What is the consequence? The prayer is still valid (sahih), but he has committed karahah tahrimiyyah (prohibitive dislike), which is sinful.
Karahah tahrimiyyah does not invalidate the prayer; it requires repetition only if done intentionally.
4
Distinguish the two levels of karahah
The text distinguishes between karahah tahrimiyyah (prohibitive dislike) and karahah tanzihiyyah (light dislike). Reciting the wajib amount removes tahrimiyyah. Reciting the sunnah amount (extra recitation) removes even tanzihiyyah.
Tanzihiyyah is not sinful but it is better to avoid.
5
Reconcile apparent contradictions
Some statements say 'leaves karahah' when reciting the wajib, while others say 'does not leave karahah'. The text reconciles this by specifying that the first refers to tahrimiyyah and the second to tanzihiyyah.
This is a common tool in Hanafi fiqh: resolving apparent contradictions by specifying the type of ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تظهر فيما ذكره لأن وجوب الإعادة حكم ترك الواجب مطلقاً لا الواجب المتأكد. وإنما يظهر في الإثم لأنه مقول بالتشكيك كما قدمناه والثلاث آيات القصار تقوم مقام السورة في الإعجاز فكذا هنا وكذا الآية الطويلة تقوم مقامها فإذا نقص عن ثلاث قصار أو آية طويلة فقد ارتكب كراهة التحريم لتركه الواجب، وإذا أتى بها خرج عن كراهة التحريم فإن قرأ القدر المسنون كما سيأتي فقد خرج عن كراهة التنزيه أيضاً وإلا فقد ارتكبها كما صرح به في شرح منية المصلي. فمن قال يخرج عن الكراهة إذا قرأ الواجب أراد التحريمية، ومن قال لا يخرج عنها أراد التنزيهية .
It appears in what he mentioned because the obligation of repeating (the prayer) is a ruling of leaving an obligatory (act) absolutely, not of a strongly emphasized obligatory. And it appears only in the sin because it is said with gradation (tashkik) as we have mentioned. And three short verses take the place of a surah in inimitability, so likewise here. And similarly a long verse takes its place. So if one falls short of three short verses or one long verse, he has committed a prohibitively disliked act (karahah tahrimiyyah) due to abandoning the obligatory. And if he recites them, he leaves the prohibitive dislike. Then if he recites the amount of sunnah as will come, he leaves the dislike of tanzih as well; otherwise, he has committed it as explicitly stated in Sharh Munyat al-Musalli. So whoever says that he leaves the dislike when he recites the obligatory means the prohibitive one, and whoever says he does not leave it means the tanzihi one.
Source pages p. 547

Commentary
This passage addresses the minimum recitation of the Qur'an in prayer and the associated levels of karahah (dislike). The author explains that the obligation to repeat a prayer is a consequence of abandoning any obligatory element, not specifically a strongly emphasized one. The concept of 'sin' here is used in a graded sense. Three short verses or one long verse count as the obligatory minimum because they equal a surah in inimitability. If one recites less than this, the prayer is valid but subject to karahah tahrimiyyah (prohibitive dislike). Reciting the obligatory amount removes this prohibitive dislike. If one also recites the recommended amount (the sunnah recitation), then even the tanzihi (lightly disliked) status is removed. The author clarifies that contradictory statements about leaving karahah are reconciled by distinguishing between the two types.

Key Terms
كراهة التحريم (karāhat al-taḥrīm): A level of dislike that is close to prohibition; the act is sinful but the prayer remains valid.
الثلاث آيات القصار (al-thalāth āyāt al-qiṣār): Three short verses, typically each approximately the length of a short verse like al-Kawthar, taken as the minimum for wajib recitation.
كراهة تنزيه (karāhat al-tanzīh): A light dislike, not sinful but better to avoid; e.g., not reciting the sunnah amount after the wajib.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the minimum recitation that removes karahah tahrimiyyah in prayer according to the Hanafi school?
A) One short verse
B) Three short verses or one long verse
C) Surah al-Fatihah only
D) Any amount of Quran

Key Takeaway
The wajib recitation in salah is three short verses or one long verse; reciting less results in karahah tahrimiyyah but the prayer is valid, while reciting the sunnah amount removes even tanzih dislike.

8 min
Saying Amin in Prayer: Ruling and Manner
Explains that both imam and ma'mum say Amin secretly, with evidence from hadith and linguistic discussion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on saying Amin in prayer, identify the evidence, and apply the correct pronunciation according to the relied-upon position.

Core Idea
Both imam and ma'mum say Amin secretly; it is a sunnah, not a Quranic verse.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on saying 'Amin' after al-Fatihah in prayer. We will explore the hadith evidence, the ruling for imam and ma'mum, and the linguistic variations in pronunciation. Understanding this ruling helps avoid common errors that may affect the validity of prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act under discussion
The act of saying 'Amin' after reciting al-Fatihah in prayer. Who is obligated? Imam, ma'mum, and munfarid.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
It is sunnah muakkadah for all, done secretly.
The hadith explicitly addresses the ma'mum, and the imam is included by indication.
3
Analyze the primary dalil
The hadith 'When the imam says Amin, then say Amin...' from Bukhari and Muslim. The imperative 'أمنوا' indicates recommendation for the ma'mum; the imam is implied.
The hadith is zanni al-thubut and zanni al-dalala.
4
Address the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
There is a weak narration from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad that the imam does not say Amin, but it is contradicted by the stronger evidence.
The author weakens it with the hadith.
5
Apply the linguistic discussion
The correct pronunciation is 'Āmīn' with madd and takhfīf. Saying with tashdid does not invalidate prayer, but erroneous forms like 'Aman' (without ya) may invalidate.
The mufta bihi is that tashdid is acceptable; the erroneous forms render the prayer invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله ( وأمن الإمام والمأموم سراً) للحديث إذا أمن الإمام فأمنوا فإنه من وافق تأمينه تأمين الملائكة غفر له ما تقدم من ذنبه (۱) رواه الشيخان وهو يفيد تأمينهما لكن في حق الإمام بالإشارة لأنه لم يسق النص له وفي حق المأموم بالعبارة لأنه سيق لأجله، وبهذا يضعف رواية الحسن عن أبي حنيفة أن الإمام لا يؤمن . وروى أبو داود وغيره أنه قال آمين وخفض بها صوته ولو قال المصنف وأمن المصلي أو الجميع كما في الحاوي القدسي لكان أولى، ليشمل المنفرد فإنه يؤمن أيضاً لرواية مسلم إذا قال أحدكم في الصلاة آمين (۲) الحديث. قال عبد الحق : في هذه الرواية اندرج المنفرد وأطلق في إخفائها فشمل الصلاة الجهرية والسرية وكل مصل لكن اختلفوا في تأمين المأموم إذا كان الإمام في السرية وسمع المأموم تامينه، منهم من قال يقوله كما هو ظاهر الكتاب، ومنهم من قال لا لأن ذلك الجهر لا عبرة به بعد الاتفاق على أنه ليست من القرآن وقد علم مما ذكرنا أن المأموم لا بقولها إلا إذا سمع قراءة الإمام لا مطلقاً فليس كالإمام مطلقاً كما هو ظاهر المختصر . وفي «آمين» أربع لغات أفصحهن وأشهرهن آمين بالمد والتخفيف والثانية بالقصر والتخفيف ومعناه استجب، والثالثة بالإمالة والرابعة بالمد والتشديد. فالأولتان مشهورتان والأخيرتان حكاهما الواحدي في أول البسيط ولهذا كان المفتى به عندنا أنه لو قال آمين بالتشديد لا تفسد لما علمت أنها لغة ولأنه موجود في القرآن ولأن له وجهاً كما قال الحلواني إن معناه ندعوك قاصدين إجابتك لأن معنى آمين قاصدين. وأنكر جماعة من مشايخنا كونها لغة وحكم بفساد الصلاة . ومن الخطأ في استعمالها «أمن» بالتشديد مع حذف الياء مقصوراً وممدوداً ولا يبعد فساد الصلاة فيهما.
His statement: (And the imam and the ma'mum say Amin secretly) due to the hadith: 'When the imam says Amin, then say Amin, for whoever's Amin coincides with the Amin of the angels, his previous sins will be forgiven.' (1) Narrated by al-Bukhari and Muslim. It conveys their saying Amin, but for the imam it is by indication because the text is not directed at him, and for the ma'mum it is explicitly because the hadith is intended for him. With this, the narration of al-Hasan from Abu Hanifa that the imam does not say Amin is weakened. And Abu Dawud and others narrated that he said Amin and lowered his voice. If the author had said 'and the one praying says Amin' or 'all of them' as in al-Hawi al-Qudsi, it would have been better to include the one praying alone, for he also says Amin based on the narration of Muslim: 'When any of you says in prayer Amin.' (2) The hadith. 'Abd al-Haqq said: In this narration, the one praying alone is included, and it is left general regarding its concealment, so it includes loud and silent prayers and every person praying. But they differed about the ma'mum saying Amin when the imam is in a silent prayer and the ma'mum hears his Amin. Some said he says it as is apparent from the book; others said no, because that loudness is not considered after agreement that it is not from the Quran. And it is known from what we have mentioned that the ma'mum only says it when he hears the recitation of the imam, not absolutely, so he is not like the imam absolutely, as is apparent from al-Mukhtasar. And 'Amin' has four linguistic forms: the most eloquent and famous is 'Amin' with elongation (madd) and lightening (takhfif); the second is with shortening (qasr) and lightening, meaning 'respond' (istajib); the third is with imalah; the fourth is with elongation and emphasis (tashdid). The first two are famous, and the last two are narrated by al-Wahidi at the beginning of al-Basit. For this reason, the operative view (mufta bihi) with us is that if one says 'Amin' with tashdid, the prayer is not invalidated because it is a known language, and because it occurs in the Quran, and because it has a meaning as al-Halwani said: it means 'we call upon You intending Your answer' because the meaning of 'Amin' is 'intending'. And a group of our scholars denied it being a language and ruled the prayer invalid. And it is an error in using it to say 'Aman' with tashdid omitting the ya, both in the shortened and lengthened forms, and the invalidation of the prayer in those two is not far-fetched.
Source pages p. 547

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling of saying 'Amin' in prayer. The author explains that both the imam and the ma'mum say it secretly, based on the well-known hadith from Bukhari and Muslim. Although the hadith explicitly addresses the ma'mum, it indicates the imam does so as well because he is the one who leads in saying it. The narration from Abu Hanifa that the imam does not say Amin is weakened by this evidence. The author prefers that the matn include the one praying alone as well, as he also says Amin. The linguistic variants of 'Amin' are discussed: the most common is with long 'a' and lightened 'm'. Saying it with tashdid (emphasis) does not invalidate the prayer according to the relied-upon position, as it is an established Arabic form. However, some early scholars considered it invalid. The author warns against the erroneous form 'Aman' (without 'ya'), which likely invalidates the prayer.

Key Terms
آمين (āmīn): A word said after reciting al-Fatihah, meaning 'O Allah, respond' (istajib).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi mufta bihi, how should the ma'mum say 'Amin' when praying behind an imam in a loud prayer?
A) Loudly, just after the imam completes Fatihah
B) Secretly, not audible to others
C) Only if the imam says it loudly
D) Only in silent prayers

Key Takeaway
Both imam and ma'mum say Amin silently; it is sunnah, and the correct pronunciation is 'Āmīn' with madd and takhfīf.

The Basmalah: Quranicity and the Ruling on Its Denier
A critical refinement of a text regarding the basmalah dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the basmalah's Quranicity and state why its denier is not deemed a disbeliever.

Core Idea
Denying the basmalah is not kufr due to a strong shubhah, but the basmalah is Quran by tawatur in the mushaf.

The basmalah is a phrase whose status has been debated among scholars. Is it part of the Quran? And if someone denies its Quranicity, does that make them a disbeliever? In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi school's nuanced answer: the basmalah is indeed Quran, but the denier is not a kafir because of a powerful doubt (shubhah) arising from early disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two sides of the dispute
State the Hanafi position (basmalah is Quran, denier is not kafir) and the opposing view (e.g., Imam Malik's claim that it is not Quran but only a sunnah).
The opposing view is attributed to Malik and some early scholars.
2
Understand the Hanafi proof from consensus
The Hanafi proof relies on the Companions' consensus to write the basmalah in the mushaf while they stripped out non-Quranic material. This consensus indicates it is Quran.
Compare with isti'adhah (a'udhu billah) which is not written in the mushaf.
3
Grasp the concept of shubhah
A strong shubhah (plausible doubt) prevents takfir even if the correct view is certain. Here, the early disagreement creates such a shubhah.
Shubhah is a key Hanafi usul principle in matters of apostasy.
4
Apply the principle to the basmalah denier
Because a reputable scholar (Malik) denied the basmalah's Quranicity, the matter is not qat'i with respect to takfir. Hence the denier is not a kafir.
This distinguishes basmalah from other Quranic verses where there is no such shubhah.
5
Note the textual correction
The author corrects the original text by dropping or adding words to resolve a contradiction about tawatur.
This is a hashiya-style refinement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبتواتر كونها قرآناً الخ» مناقض لقوله «فالموجب الخ». وعلى نسخة وبعدم تواتر» مناقض لقوله وهو دليل تواتر كونها قرآناً كما لا يخفى والصواب في تقرير هذا المحل ما ذكره المحقق ابن الهمام في كتابه التحرير وهو أن القطعي إنما يكفر منكره إذا لم تثبت فيه شبهة قوية كإنكار ركن، وهنا قد وجدت وذلك لأن من أنكرها كمالك ادعى عدم تواتر كونها قرآناً في الأوائل وأن كتابتها فيها لشهرة استنان الافتتاح بها في الشرع والآخر يقول : إجماعهم على كتابتها مع أمرهم بتجريد المصاحف يوجب كونها قرآناً والاستنان لا يسوغ الإجماع لتحققه في الاستعاذة والأحق أنها من القرآن لتواترها في المصحف وهو دليل كونها قرآناً، ولا نسلم توقف ثبوت القرآنية على تواتر الأخبار بكونها قرآناً بل الشرط فيما هو قرآن تواتره في محله فقط وإن لم يتواتر كونه في محله من القرآن اهـ. وقوله «ولا نسلم ردّ لما تضمنه كلام المنكر من أن تواترها في محلها لا يستلزم قرآنيتها بل لا بد من تواتر الأخبار بكونها قرآناً. والحاصل أن تواترها في محلها أثبت أصل قرآنيتها، وأما كونها قرآناً متواتراً فهو متوقف على تواتر الأخبار به ولذلك لم يكفر منكرها بخلاف غيرها لتواتر الأخبار بقرآنيته، وقد ظهر لك من هذا التقرير الشافي أن ما ذكره في شرح المنار صحيح موافق لما قلنا وأما ما ذكره هنا فلا لما علمت وتصحيحه بإسقاط قوله تواتر ) من قوله وهو دليل تواتر كونها قرآناً» وبإسقاط قوله «وبه اندفعت الشبهة وبزيادة لفظة «عدم» في قوله وبتواتر كونها قرآناً كما مر والله سبحانه ولي التوفيق.
And the phrase 'with tawatur of it being Quran' contradicts his statement 'so the obligating etc.' And on the version 'and without tawatur' contradicts his statement which is evidence of tawatur of it being Quran, as is not hidden. And the correct way in explaining this place is what the investigator Ibn al-Humam mentioned in his book al-Tahrir: namely, that the definitive (qat'i) matter only makes its denier a disbeliever if no strong suspicion (shubhah) is established, like denial of a pillar. And here a strong suspicion has occurred, because the denier, like Malik, claimed the lack of tawatur of it being Quran in the early times, and that its writing in it (the mushaf) is due to the fame of the sunnah of opening with it in the Shari'ah. And the other (the proponent) says: The consensus of the Companions on writing it along with their command to strip the mushafs (of non-Quranic material) necessitates it being Quran. And the sunnah does not justify consensus, because it (consensus) is realized in the case of isti'adhah. And the most correct is that it is from the Quran due to its tawatur in the mushaf, which is evidence of it being Quran. And we do not accept that the establishment of being Quran is dependent on the tawatur of reports that it is Quran; rather, the condition for something being Quran is its tawatur in its place (in the mushaf) only, even if its being in that place of the Quran is not transmitted by tawatur. End. And his statement 'we do not accept' is a rebuttal of what the denier's statement implies: that its tawatur in its place does not necessitate its being Quran, but rather there must be tawatur of reports that it is Quran. In summary, its tawatur in its place established its original being Quran. As for it being Quran in a mutawatir sense, it is dependent on tawatur of reports about it. Therefore, its denier is not declared a disbeliever, unlike others, due to the tawatur of reports about its being Quran. And it has become clear to you from this sufficient clarification that what he mentioned in Sharh al-Manar is correct and agrees with what we said. As for what he mentioned here, it is not, due to what you know. And its correction is by dropping the word 'tawatur' from his statement 'which is evidence of tawatur of it being Quran' and by dropping his statement 'and with it the suspicion is removed' and by adding the word 'lack' ('adam) in his statement 'and with tawatur of it being Quran' as has passed. And Allah is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 547

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hāshiya) addressing a contradiction in the text regarding the basmalah. It discusses the dispute over whether the basmalah is part of the Quran and whether denying it constitutes kufr. The author cites Ibn al-Humam's view: a definitive (qatʿī) matter only makes its denier a kāfir if there is no strong shubhah (doubt). Here, a strong shubhah exists because early scholars like Imam Malik denied its Qur'anicity, claiming it was not transmitted mutawatir as Quran but only written in the mushaf because of the established practice of opening with it. The proponents argue that the consensus of the Companions on writing it, while they stripped the mushafs of non-Quranic additions, proves it is Quran. The correct view is that tawatur in the mushaf establishes its original being Quran; however, its tawatur as Quran (i.e., transmitted reports that it is Quran) is not necessary for its being Quran. Consequently, one who denies it is not a kāfir, unlike denial of other clearly mutawatir verses. The author then corrects the phrasing in the original text.

Key Terms
تواتر (tawātur): Multiple chains of transmission reaching a level of certainty; here, the basmalah is present in the mushaf in a universally transmitted manner, proving its Quranic status according to some.
شبهة (shubhah): A plausible doubt or ambiguity that prevents a definitive ruling of disbelief even if the matter is certain.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school hold that one who denies the basmalah is from the Qur'an is not a disbeliever?
A) Because basmalah is not actually part of the Quran
B) Because there is a strong shubhah (doubt) from early scholarly disagreement
C) Because the basmalah is only a sunnah, not a verse
D) Because the Qur'an is not mutawatir

Key Takeaway
The basmalah is Quran, but its denier is not a kafir due to a strong shubhah from early ikhtilaf.

Takbir Without Elongation When Bowing and Its Legal Consequences
States the ruling of saying takbir when going into ruku' without elongating the vowel, citing hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the pronunciation of takbir when moving to ruku', and apply the prohibition of elongation to determine its legal effect.

Core Idea
Takbir must be said without vowel elongation; deliberate distortion may invalidate the prayer.

When we move to bow in prayer, we say 'Allahu Akbar'. But how we say it matters. The Hanafi school emphasises that the takbir should be pronounced crisply, without stretching the vowel of 'Allah'. This lesson unpacks the ruling, the prophetic proof, and the serious consequence of intentional elongation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is saying takbir when moving to ruku'. Its ruling is wajib. The specific condition here is that it must be said without elongation (madd).
The takbir itself is wajib; elongation is a prohibitive element that can affect validity.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites the hadith from Abu Hurairah in Sahih Bukhari and Muslim, which describes the Prophet's prayer sequence including takbir at each movement.
The hadith is mutawatir in meaning (thubut qat'i) but the instruction 'without elongation' is derived from practice.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith establishes the place and occurrence of takbir. The phrase 'al-takbiru jarmun' (the takbir is a cutting) indicates that the letters should be pronounced distinctly without stretching. This is the basis for the prohibition of madd.
The derivation is from the Prophet's practice and the linguistic understanding of 'jarm'.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Other schools may permit slight elongation, but the Hanafi position is strict. The text from al-Mabsut states that elongating the alif of Allah could lead to kufr if done intentionally.
The severity depends on intent and whether the meaning changes.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is to avoid any elongation. Al-Mabsut is a primary source, and later works uphold this view.
The ruling is consistent across Hanafi manuals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله (وكبر بلا مد وركع لما في الصحيحين عن أبي هريرة قال : كان رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم إذا قام إلى الصلاة يكبر حين يقوم ثم يكبر حين يركع، ثم يقول سمع الله لمن حمده حين يرفع صلبه من الركوع، ثم يقول وهو قائم ربنا ولك الحمد، ثم يكبر حين يهوي ساجداً، ثم يكبر حين يرفع رأسه، ثم يفعل ذلك في الصلاة كلها حتى يقضها ويكبر حين يقوم من الثنتين بعد الجلوس معنى قوله بلا مد حذفه من غير تطويل وهو معنى ما ورد «التكبير جرم». وصاحله الإمساك عن إشباع الحركة والتعمق فيها والإضراب عن الهمزة المفرطة والمد الفاحش. وفي المبسوط : لو مد ألف الله لا يصير شارعاً وخيف عليه الكفر إن كان قاصداً.
His statement: (And he should say takbir without elongation and bow) due to what is in the two Sahihs from Abu Hurairah who said: 'The Messenger of Allah (SAW) used to, when he stood for prayer, say takbir when he stood, then say takbir when he bowed, then say Sami'a Allahu liman hamidah when he raised his back from bowing, then say while standing Rabbana wa laka al-hamd, then say takbir when he prostrated, then say takbir when he raised his head, then do that throughout the prayer until he finished it, and he said takbir when he stood from the two (rak'ahs) after sitting.' The meaning of his statement 'without elongation' is to deliver it without prolongation, which is the meaning of what is narrated: 'The takbir is a cutting' (al-takbiru jarmun). And its correct way is to refrain from exaggerating the vowel and becoming excessive in it, and to avoid the exaggerated hamzah and excessive elongation. And in al-Mabsut: If one elongates the alif of Allah, he does not become a participant (in the prayer), and it is feared that he commits disbelief if he intended (that).
Source pages p. 548

Commentary
This passage addresses the proper pronunciation of the takbir when moving to ruku'. The author cites the famous hadith from Abu Hurairah describing the Prophet's (SAW) actions in prayer, which includes saying takbir at each movement. The key point is that the takbir should be said 'without elongation' (بلا مد), meaning one should not prolong the vowel of 'Allah'. According to al-Mabsut, elongating the alif of Allah could imply a different meaning, and if done intentionally, it might lead to disbelief (kufr) because it changes the name of Allah. The phrase 'التكبير جرم' (the takbir is a cutting) indicates that the letters should be pronounced distinctly and swiftly, without stretching.

Key Terms
بلا مد (bilā madd): Without elongation of the vowel, specifically the alif in 'Allah' should not be lengthened excessively.
التكبير جرم (al-takbīru jarmun): A statement meaning that the takbir should be pronounced with distinct, separated letters without blending or stretching.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Permissible and does not affect the prayer
B) Prohibitively disliked and could lead to kufr if deliberate and intended
C) Obligatory to elongate it
D) Only disliked in loud prayers

Key Takeaway
The takbir must be pronounced without elongation; deliberately distorting the name of Allah can invalidate the prayer and even constitute kufr.

Ruling on Saying 'Amin' with Tashdid in Prayer
Explaining the ruling on the follower saying 'amin' and the various linguistic forms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the follower saying 'Amin' and evaluate the validity of saying 'Amin' with tashdid based on linguistic evidence.

Core Idea
Saying 'Amin' with tashdid does not invalidate prayer; it is a valid Arabic language.

This lesson addresses two key fiqh questions: when should a follower say 'Amin' in prayer, and does pronouncing it with tashdid invalidate the prayer? The Hanafi school has internal disagreements on both points, and we explore the linguistic and textual evidence that leads to the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
State the follower's position on saying 'Amin'
Identify when the follower says 'Amin' according to the Hanafi school: only when hearing the imam's recitation, not absolutely.
2
Identify the disagreement among Hanafi scholars
Note that some scholars argue the follower should not say 'Amin' in silent prayers even if he hears the imam, because it is not Quran.
3
List the four linguistic forms of 'Amin'
Recognize the four pronunciations: with madd + tashdid, madd + no tashdid, qasr + no tashdid, imala.
4
Analyze the dispute on 'Amin' with tashdid
Understand that some scholars rule prayer invalid if said with tashdid, while others (mufta bihi) say it is valid because it is a known language and exists in Quranic reading.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and the erroneous forms
State the relied-upon position: prayer is valid even with tashdid. Also note the error of omitting ya (making 'Amman' or 'Amman') which likely invalidates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حديث. قال عبد الحق : في هذه الرواية اندرج المنفرد وأطلق في إخفائها فشمل الصلاة الجهرية والسرية وكل مصل لكن اختلفوا في تأمين المأموم إذا كان الإمام في السرية وسمع المأموم تامينه، منهم من قال يقوله كما هو هو ظاهر الكتاب، ومنهم من قال لا لأن ذلك الجهر لا عبرة به بعد الاتفاق على أنه ليست من القرآن وقد علم مما ذكرنا أن المأموم لا يقولها إلا إذا سمع قراءة الإمام لا مطلقاً فليس كالإمام مطلقاً كما هو ظاهر المختصر . وفي «آمين» أربع لغات أفصحهن وأشهرهن آمين بالمد والتخفيف والثانية بالقصر والتخفيف ومعناه استجب، والثالثة بالإمالة والرابعة بالمد والتشديد. فالأولتان مشهورتان والأخيرتان حكاهما الواحدي في أول البسيط ولهذا كان المفتى به عندنا أنه لو قال آمين بالتشديد لا تفسد لما علمت أنها لغة ولأنه موجود في القرآن ولأن له وجهاً كما قال الحلواني إن معناه ندعوك قاصدين إجابتك لأن معنى آمين قاصدين. وأنكر جماعة من مشايخنا كونها لغة وحكم بفساد الصلاة . ومن الخطأ في استعمالها «أمن» بالتشديد مع حذف الياء مقصوراً وممدوداً ولا يبعد فساد الصلاة فيهما.
Hadith. Abd al-Haqq said: In this narration, the one praying alone is included and in its concealment it is general, so it includes jahri and sirri prayers and every prayer. But they differed regarding the follower's saying of 'amin' when the imam is in sirri prayer and the follower hears his 'amin'. Some said he says it, as is apparent from the book. Some said no, because that jahr is not considered after agreement that it is not from the Quran. It has become known from what we mentioned that the follower does not say it except when he hears the imam's recitation, not absolutely, so he is not like the imam absolutely, as is apparent from al-Mukhtasar. For 'Amin' there are four languages: the most eloquent and famous is 'Amin' with lengthening and without emphasis, the second with shortening and without emphasis meaning 'accept', the third with incline (imala), and the fourth with lengthening and emphasis (tashdid). The first two are famous, and the last two were narrated by al-Wahidi in the beginning of al-Basit. For this reason, the mufta bihi according to us is that if one says 'Amin' with tashdid, his prayer is not invalidated because it is known that it is a language, and because it exists in the Quran, and because it has a meaning as al-Hilwani said: its meaning is 'we call you intending your response', because the meaning of 'Amin' is 'intending'. Some of our scholars denied it being a language and ruled the prayer invalid. And it is an error in its usage to say 'Amin' with tashdid with omission of the ya', shortened or lengthened, and it is not unlikely that the prayer is invalidated in both cases.
Source pages p. 548

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the word 'Amin' and the follower's saying it in prayer. The text begins by noting a disagreement among Hanafi scholars whether the follower (ma'mum) says 'Amin' when the imam is reciting silently (sirri) and the follower hears the imam's 'Amin'. Some say yes, following the apparent meaning of the matn; others say no because such audible utterance is irrelevant since it is not Quran. The author clarifies the relied-upon position: the follower only says 'Amin' when he hears the imam's recitation, not absolutely. Then the text details the four linguistic forms of 'Amin': with madd and tashdid, with qasr and without tashdid, with imala, and with madd and tashdid. The mufta bihi is that saying 'Amin' with tashdid does not invalidate the prayer, because it is a valid language, exists in Quranic reading, and has a sound meaning. However, some Hanafi scholars (a group of our mashayikh) reject it as a language and rule the prayer invalid. The text also warns that saying 'Amin' with tashdid while omitting the ya (making it 'Amman' or 'Amman') is erroneous and likely invalidates the prayer. This lesson demonstrates the Hanafi methodology of preferring a ruling based on linguistic and Quranic evidence despite scholarly disagreement.

Key Terms
آمين (amin): A word said after al-Fatiha meaning 'O Allah, respond' or 'accept'.
التأمين (al-ta'min): The act of saying 'Amin' in prayer.
المد (al-madd): Lengthening the vowel sound; in 'Amin' it can be with madd and tashdid or without.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi in the Hanafi school, if a person says 'Amin' with tashdid (emphasis) in prayer, what is the ruling?
A) The prayer becomes invalid.
B) The prayer is valid because it is a correct language.
C) It is makruh and invalidates the prayer.
D) It is wajib to repeat the prayer.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, saying 'Amin' with tashdid does not invalidate the prayer; it is a valid linguistic form.

Rulings on Lengthening (Madd) in the Opening Takbir
Detailed analysis of madd in takbir with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the rulings for lengthening at the beginning, middle, and end of 'Allah' and 'Akbar' in the takbir, and apply them to determine the validity of prayer entry (shuru').

Core Idea
Lengthening at the beginning of 'Allah' or 'Akbar' invalidates shuru'; middle lengthening is makruh unless exaggerated; end lengthening is a mistake but valid.

The opening takbir is the key to entering prayer. The Prophet ﷺ said it succinctly without extension. But what if someone lengthens a letter? The Hanafi school provides a detailed linguistic analysis, distinguishing between positions and intentions. This lesson unpacks that ruling from the classical text of Bahr al-Raiq, based on the hadith of Abu Hurayra.

Guided Path
1
Identify the foundational hadith
The passage begins by citing the hadith of Abu Hurayra (in Bukhari and Muslim) describing the Prophet's concise takbir. This establishes the sunnah: no undue lengthening.
The hadith is the proof for the instruction 'say takbir without lengthening'.
2
Classify the positions of madd in 'Allah'
The text then analyzes lengthening at the beginning (hamza al-wasl), middle (between lam and ha), and end (on the ha) of the word 'Allah'.
Each position has a different ruling: beginning invalidates, middle is correct unless exaggerated, end is a mistake.
3
Apply the same classification to 'Akbar'
Similarly, lengthening at the beginning (hamza), middle (between kaf and ba), and end (on the ra) of 'Akbar' is examined.
Analogous rulings apply, with additional considerations about the word becoming 'Akbar' (i.e., 'Ikkar' meaning old or Satan).
4
Evaluate the ikhtilaf on kufr
A key dispute: does intentional lengthening of the hamza of 'Allah' cause kufr? Some say yes if it introduces doubt (taqrir hamza). Others say no if certain of meaning.
The mufta bihi is that kufr is not declared lightly; it depends on doubt about the meaning.
5
Determine the operative ruling for fatwa
Based on the text and later authorities, the relied-upon position: lengthening at the beginning of 'Allah' or 'Akbar' invalidates prayer; middle is makruh if exaggerated, else valid; end is a mistake but valid.
The passage criticizes a view that would declare kufr easily; leniency is preferred.

إذا تعمده؟ قيل نعم للشك، وقيل لا . ولا ينبغي أنه لا أن يختلف في الشروع به. وإن كان يصح في وسطه حتى صار أكبار» لا يصير شارعاً. وإن قال في خلال الصلاة تفسد. وفي زلة القارىء للصدر الشهيد يصير شارعاً لكن ينبغي أن يكون هذا مقيداً بما إذا لم يقصد به المخالفة كما نبه عليه محمد بن مقاتل، وإن كان في آخرة فقد قيل تفسد صلاته وقياسه أن لا الشروع به أيضاً. كذا يصح في شرح الأستاذ على الهدية عن شرح المنية لابن على منفي لما أنه حمل المخاطب على الإقرار بأمر قد استقر عنده ثبوته أو نفيه، بل أغلب أحواله دخوله على المثبت، ولذا أولوا التقرير في ألم نشرح» بما بعد النفي والهمزة فيها ليست في التحقيق إلا للإنكار الإيطالي وإنكار النفي نفي له ونفي النفي إثبات، ومثله قوله تعالى أليس الله بکاف عبده ﴾ [الزمر: ٣٦]. قوله : (أو باءه) قال في النهر : وفي القنية: لا تفسد لأنه أشباع وهو لغة قوم، واستبعده الشارح بأنه لا يجوز إلا في الشعر، وقيل هو جمع كبير. وفي
His saying: (And say the takbir without lengthening and bow) because of what is in the two Sahihs from Abu Hurayra: When the Messenger of Allah stood for prayer, he would say the takbir when standing, then say the takbir when bowing, then say 'Sami'a Allahu liman hamidah' when raising his back from bowing, then say while standing 'Rabbana wa laka al-hamd', then say the takbir when prostrating, then say the takbir when raising his head, and he did that throughout the prayer until he finished it, and he would say the takbir when standing from the two [rak'ats] after sitting. The meaning of his saying 'without lengthening' is to say it without prolongation, and its meaning is what is narrated: 'Takbir is jarm'. And its meaning is to refrain from exaggerating the movement and going deep in it and to avoid exaggerated hamza and excessive lengthening. In al-Mabsut: If one lengthens the alif of 'Allah', he does not become a shar'i (one who enters prayer) and it is feared upon him disbelief if he intended. This decisive explanation: What was mentioned in Sharh al-Manar is correct and agrees with what we said; as for what was mentioned here, it is not, as you have learned. Its correction is by dropping his word 'tawatur' from his saying 'and it is proof of tawatur that it is Quran' and by dropping his word 'and with it the doubt is removed' and by adding the word 'adam' in his saying 'and by tawatur that it is Quran' as passed. Allah is the granter of success. His saying: (And it has become known from what we mentioned etc.) i.e. because if he does not hear the recitation from the imam in jahri prayer, he does not know the time of his 'amin'. As the author of al-Majma' stated in his commentary: After mentioning the hadith of the two Shaykhs: The knowledge of the imam's saying 'amin' is obtained by finishing al-Fatiha, so the attachment to the known saying is valid even if not heard. But in al-Jawhara: If the muqtadi hears the ta'min from another muqtadi in Jumu'ah and 'Eid, Imam Zahir al-Din said: He says amin. Similarly in al-Fatawa. He said in al-Sharnublaliyya: I say, on this basis it should not be specific to these two, but the ruling applies to large congregations likewise. i.e. because the purpose is that he was far from the imam, not hearing the imam's recitation, he heard the ta'min of the muqtadi who is with him hearing the imam's recitation, so he also says amin because the purpose is the knowledge of the existence of the imam's ta'min. His saying: (And in al-Mabsut: If one lengthens the alif of Allah etc.) Know that lengthening is either in 'Allah' or in 'Akbar'. Likewise, if he lengthens the alif of 'Akbar' or its ba', he does not become a shar'i because 'Ikkar' is the plural of 'Kibar' meaning 'old' or it is said a name of Satan. If he lengthens the 'Allahu' it is a linguistic error; likewise if he lengthens its ra'. Lengthening the lam of 'Allah' is correct; stopping (sakt) on the ha' is wrong because it only occurs in poetic necessity. Al-Akmal discussed in al-‘Inaya regarding their saying that if he lengthens the hamza of 'Allah', the prayer is invalid and he becomes a disbeliever if he does it intentionally due to doubt, because the hamza could be for interrogation (taqrir) so there is neither disbelief nor invalidation. There is a problem in this because Ibn Hisham in al-Mughni said: The fourth [type of hamza] is taqrir, meaning to make the addressee admit and confess to a matter that is established in his mind as affirmative or negative, and it must be followed by the thing being confirmed. You say in taqrir with a verb: 'Did you hit Zayd?' or 'Is it you who hit Zayd?' or with an object: 'Is it Zayd whom you hit?' as is required in questioning. And 'Allahu Akbar' is not from 'Akbar'? If it is in 'Allah', then it is either at its beginning, middle, or end. If it is at its beginning, it invalidates the prayer and one does not become a shar'i with it; if one cannot distinguish between them, he does not become a disbeliever because disbelief is based on doubt about the content of the sentence; if he is certain, there is no disbelief. If it is in its middle, it is correct except that one should not exaggerate; if he exaggerates until an alif occurs between the lam and ha', it is makruh. It is said: The chosen view is that it does not invalidate, and it is not far-fetched. If it is at its end, it is a mistake but also does not invalidate; and by analogy of non-invalidation in both, beginning (shuru') with it is valid. If the lengthening is in 'Akbar', if it is at its beginning, it is a mistake that invalidates prayer; does he become a disbeliever if intentional? It is said yes due to doubt, and it is said no. And it is not appropriate that there is difference in beginning with it. If it is correct in its middle so that it becomes 'Akbar' (with a), he does not become a shar'i. If he says it during the prayer, it invalidates. In Zillat al-Qari' by al-Sadr al-Shahid, he becomes a shar'i, but this should be conditioned that he does not intend opposition, as Muhammad b. Muqatil noted. If it is at its end, it is said his prayer is invalidated, and by analogy, beginning with it is also not valid. This is correct in Sharh al-Ustadh on al-Hadiyya from Sharh al-Munya by Ibn Amir Haj. His saying: (And it is feared upon him disbelief if he intended) Some virtuous people said: It appears that mere intention to lengthen the hamza does not cause disbelief, but rather if he intends the meaning, i.e. interrogation implying prior doubt. And its equivalent was stated in Sharh al-Munya. In Sharh al-Mi'raj after quoting from al-Khulasa: If one lengthens the alif of 'Akbar', they discussed his disbelief and his prayer is not valid, its text: 'Because if disbelief is required, it is obvious; otherwise it is speech containing possibility of disbelief, so disbelief is feared upon him, and it is also a legal mistake because the hamza when entering a negated statement as in a 'alam nashrah is for taqrir, not in an affirmative statement.' So it was said. Also af'al al-tafdil (elative) does not accept lengthening. He said in al-Nahr: The weakness of this statement is not hidden from you, as taqrir does not require entering a negated statement, because it makes the addressee admit to a matter established as affirmative or negative; rather its common occurrence is on affirmative statements. Therefore, they interpreted taqrir in 'alam nashrah' as following the negation, and the hamza in it is actually only for rhetorical negation, and negation of negation is affirmation. Similarly, His statement 'a laysa Allahu bi kafi 'abdihi' (39:36). His saying: (or its ba') He said in al-Nahr: In al-Qunya: It does not invalidate because it is merely lengthening (ishba') and it is a language of some people. The commentator considered it far-fetched because it is only permissible in poetry. It is said it is the plural of kabir.
Source pages p. 548

Commentary
This passage is a multi-layered commentary on the ruling regarding lengthening (madd) in the takbir (saying 'Allahu Akbar') when entering prayer. The text begins by explaining the instruction 'say the takbir without lengthening' based on the hadith of Abu Hurayra describing the Prophet's prayer. The commentary then dives into a detailed analysis of what happens if one lengthens the alif of 'Allah' or 'Akbar'. It distinguishes between lengthening at the beginning, middle, or end of the word, and the consequences: some changes may invalidate the prayer, some may prevent valid entrance into prayer (shuru'), and some may even incur disbelief if done intentionally with doubt. The discussion involves linguistic analysis (e.g., the hamza of taqrir) and cites authorities like al-Mabsut, al-‘Inaya, and Ibn Hisham. The text also addresses a scenario where the follower says 'amin' based on hearing it from another follower in a large congregation, extending the ruling beyond Jumu'ah and 'Eid. The overall purpose is to clarify the precise boundaries of valid takbir and the severity of deviations.

Key Terms
المد (al-madd): Lengthening of a vowel sound; in the takbir, it refers to lengthening the alif in 'Allah' or 'Akbar'.
الشروع (al-shuru'): Entering into the prayer by saying the opening takbir.
التقرير (al-taqrir): A type of interrogative hamza that prompts confirmation, not genuine questioning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if a person lengthens the lam of 'Allah' in the takbir, causing an extra alif between the lam and ha?
A) The prayer is invalid and shuru' does not occur.
B) It is makruh but the prayer is valid.
C) It is a mistake but does not invalidate the prayer.
D) It is correct and no issue.

Key Takeaway
The takbir must be pronounced correctly; major deviations at the beginning prevent entry into prayer, while middle and end errors are less severe and do not invalidate unless they change the meaning.

Mistakes in Pronouncing the Takbir
Rulings on mispronouncing takbir

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on mispronouncing 'Allahu Akbar' for each type of mistake (prolongation of letters in 'Allah' vs 'Akbar'), and identify whether it invalidates prayer, necessitates kufr, or is permissible.

Core Idea
A mispronunciation of takbir only invalidates prayer or causes kufr if it implies doubt or changes the meaning to something kufr.

The takbir 'Allahu Akbar' is the opening phrase of prayer, and its correct pronunciation is essential. But what happens if someone inadvertently or intentionally prolongs a letter? This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explains the nuanced Hanafi position: not every mistake invalidates prayer or leads to disbelief. The key factor is whether the mistake introduces doubt (shakk) or changes the meaning to something kufr (like 'ikbar' meaning vanity). Let's analyze the different scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of mistake
Determine which letter (alif, ba, lam, ha, etc.) is prolonged and whether it is in 'Allah' or 'Akbar'. The passage distinguishes between mistakes in 'Allah' and mistakes in 'Akbar'.
Also note whether the mistake is at the beginning, middle, or end of the word.
2
Assess the linguistic and legal consequence
For each mistake, check whether it is a linguistic error, and whether it affects the meaning (e.g., 'Ikbar' vs 'Akbar'). If the meaning changes to something invalid (like vanity or a name of Satan), then starting prayer with it is not valid.
If the meaning remains unchanged, it may still be a linguistic mistake but not invalidate prayer.
3
Determine invalidity of prayer
The passage states that mistakes at the beginning of 'Allah' or 'Akbar' invalidate the prayer (i.e., the takbir is not valid for starting prayer). Middle and end mistakes generally do not invalidate, unless they cause an alif between letters (in 'Allah') which is makruh, or they change the word to 'Akbar' (middle of 'Akbar' invalidates starting).
Differentiate between invalidity of the takbir itself and invalidity of the entire prayer.
4
Evaluate the ruling on kufr (disbelief)
Kufr only applies if the person intentionally prolongs while having doubt (shakk) about the meaning. If they are certain of the correct meaning, there is no kufr. The relied-upon view is that mere prolongation does not cause kufr unless it implies a questioning or doubting attitude.
This is the mufta bihi: no kufr without doubt.
5
Apply the mufta bihi ruling
The final operative ruling: simple lengthening of the alif of 'Akbar' does not invalidate prayer and does not cause kufr, unless the person intends doubt or the meaning becomes something else.
If there is disagreement (e.g., some said it invalidates), the later scholars (Ibn Amir Haj, al-Nahr) prefer the view of non-invalidation.

النفي نفي له ونفي النفي إثبات، ومثله قوله تعالى ﴿أليس الله بكاف عبده﴾ [الزمر: ٣٦]. قوله : (أو باءه) قال في النهر : وفي القنية: لا تفسد لأنه إشباع وهو لغة قوم، واستبعده الشارح بأنه لا يجوز إلا في الشعر، وقيل هو جمع كبير. وفي الخلاصة. هذا القبيل إذ ليس هنا مخاطب كما لا يخفى لكن ذكر في المطول أن التقرير يقال على التحقق والثبوت ويقال على حملك المخاطب إلى آخره ولعل الأكمل أراد المعنى الأول.
Likewise if one prolongs the alif of 'Akbar' or its ba, he does not become a starter [of prayer] because 'Ikbar' is a plural of 'Kibar' which is vanity, and it is said a name of Satan. If one prolongs the alif of 'Allah', it is a linguistic mistake, likewise if one prolongs its ra and prolonging the lam of Allah is correct, and stopping with sukun on the ha is a mistake because it only occurs in poetic necessity. Al-Akmal discussed in Al-'Inayah regarding their statement that if one prolongs the hamza of 'Allah', the prayer is invalidated and he becomes a disbeliever if he does it intentionally due to doubt, because the hamza could be for affirmation so there is no disbelief or invalidity. He said: There is consideration [of this] because Ibn Hisham said in Al-Mughni: The fourth [type] is affirmation (taqrir) and its meaning is making the addressee acknowledge and confess a matter that is established in his mind as existent or non-existent, and it must be followed by the thing affirmed. You say in affirmation of an action: 'Did you hit Zayd?' or of the subject: 'Was it you who hit Zayd?' or of the object: 'Was it Zayd you hit?' as is required in interrogation. And 'Allahu Akbar' is not of that. If [the mistake] is in 'Allah', then it is either at its beginning, middle, or end. If at the beginning, it invalidates the prayer and he does not become a starter thereby. If he cannot distinguish between them, he does not become a disbeliever because disbelief is based on him being doubtful about the content of this sentence, so when he is certain, there is no disbelief. If in the middle, it is correct except that one should not exaggerate it; if he exaggerates until from its lengthening an alif occurs between the lam and ha, it is disliked. It is said: The chosen view is that it does not invalidate, and it is not far. If at the end, it is a mistake and also does not invalidate, and by analogy of non-invalidation in both, becoming a starter by them is valid. If the lengthening is in 'Akbar', then if at its beginning, it is a mistake that invalidates the prayer. Does he become a disbeliever if intentional? It is said yes due to doubt, and it is said no. There should be no disagreement about starting with it. If in the middle so that it becomes 'Akbar', he does not become a starter. If he says it during the prayer, it invalidates. In Zillat al-Qari' by al-Sadr al-Shahid, he becomes a starter, but this should be restricted to when he does not intend opposition, as Muhammad ibn Muqatil noted. If at the end, it is said his prayer is invalidated, and by analogy starting with it is also invalid. Likewise in Sharh al-Ustadh 'ala al-Hidya from Sharh al-Munya by Ibn Amir Haj. His statement: 'And it is feared for him disbelief if he intends it.' Some virtuous scholars said: The apparent is that merely intending to prolong the hamza does not cause disbelief, but rather if he intends the meaning which is interrogation implying prior doubt. H. And its like preceded from Sharh al-Munya. In Sharh al-Mi'raj after quoting from al-Khulasa: 'If one prolongs the alif of 'Akbar', they discussed his disbelief and his prayer is not valid.' Its text: 'Because if disbelief necessarily follows, it is apparent; otherwise it is speech containing possibility of disbelief so it is feared for him disbelief, and it is also a mistake legally because the hamza when it enters a negated phrase as in the verse 'Alam nashrah' is for affirmation, not in an apparently positive phrase. So it is said, and also the elative does not accept prolongation.' He said in al-Nahr: 'It is not hidden from you the weakness of this statement, for affirmation is not conditional upon entering a negative because it is making the addressee acknowledge a matter established in his mind as existent or non-existent; rather most of its occurrences enter a positive. Therefore they interpreted affirmation in 'Alam nashrah' as after the negation, and the hamza there is actually only for denial of precedent, and denial of negation is a negation, and negation of negation is affirmation.' And similar is the verse 'Alaysa Allahu bi kafin 'abdah' (Az-Zumar: 36). His statement: 'Or its ba'. He said in al-Nahr: In al-Qunya: It does not invalidate because it is a prolongation which is a dialect of some people. The commentator deemed it unlikely because it is only permissible in poetry. And it is said it is a plural of 'kabir'. And in al-Khulasa: This type since there is no addressee here, as is not hidden, but it is mentioned in al-Mutawwal that 'taqrir' is used for verification and establishment, and for making the addressee acknowledge etc. Perhaps al-Akmal intended the first meaning.
Source pages p. 549

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling on mistakes in pronouncing the takbir (الله أكبر) and their effects on the validity of prayer and potential disbelief. It begins by stating that if one prolongs the alif of 'أكبر' or its 'ba', he does not become a starter of prayer, meaning the takbir is not valid for initiating prayer. The reason given is that 'إكبار' (if read with kasra) is a plural of 'كبر' meaning vanity, or a name of Satan. Prolonging the alif of 'الله' is a linguistic mistake. The commentator then discusses a view that if one intentionally prolongs the hamza of 'الله' due to doubt, his prayer is invalid and he becomes a disbeliever. Al-Akmal in al-'Inayah questions this, arguing that the hamza could be for affirmation (taqrir), so there is no disbelief. Ibn Hisham's definition of taqrir is cited, and it is concluded that 'الله أكبر' is not of that type. The passage then categorizes mistakes in 'الله' by position: at the beginning it invalidates prayer and one does not become a starter; in the middle it is correct unless excessive; at the end it is a mistake but does not invalidate. Similarly for 'أكبر': at the beginning it invalidates; in the middle (becoming 'أكبار') it does not effect starting; at the end there is disagreement. The text also discusses the ruling of kufr: it depends on doubt. The later part addresses a more nuanced view from al-Nahr that taqrir can enter positive statements, weakening the earlier objection. This is a classic example of Hanafi legal reasoning in the muta'akhirin period, synthesizing multiple authorities to arrive at the mufta bihi position that mere lengthening of the alif does not necessarily cause kufr if there is no doubt.

Key Terms
تقرير (taqrir): A type of interrogative hamza that makes the addressee acknowledge an established fact; it is not a question seeking information.
شك (shakk): Doubt about the meaning of the takbir; if present, intentional mispronunciation may lead to kufr.
إكبار (ikbar): A plural of 'kibar' meaning vanity, or a name of Satan; if one intends this meaning, it invalidates prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi view in this passage, what is the ruling on a person who intentionally prolongs the 'alif' of 'Akbar' in the opening takbir without any doubt about the meaning?
A) The prayer is invalid and he becomes a disbeliever.
B) The prayer is valid and he does not become a disbeliever.
C) The prayer is invalid but he does not become a disbeliever.
D) The prayer is valid but he must repeat the takbir.

Key Takeaway
A mispronunciation of takbir only affects the validity of prayer or leads to disbelief if it changes the meaning to kufr or reflects doubt; simple lengthening without doubt is excused.

Sunnahs of Ruku' Posture and Tasbih
Describes the sunnahs of ruku' from hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the sunnah actions of ruku' and distinguish them from the obligatory components of the prayer.

Core Idea
Ruku' itself is a pillar; its described postures and tasbih are sunnah, not wajib.

This lesson details the prophetic practice (sunnah) during the ruku' posture. Understanding these practices helps perfect one's prayer without confusing them with obligatory elements.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the pillar from the sunnah
Recognize that the act of bowing (ruku') is a fard pillar; the specific ways of performing it are sunnah.
The minimal ruku' suffices for validity; the sunnah actions add perfection.
2
List the sunnah postures
Identify each sunnah: placing hands on knees with spread fingers, stretching the back, keeping head level with buttocks, bringing heels together, and facing toes toward qibla.
These are based on authentic hadith.
3
Note the abrogated practice
Tathiq (placing palms between thighs) is abrogated; the sunnah is hands on knees.
Cited from Ibn Mas'ud but overridden.
4
Understand the tasbih ruling
Saying 'Subhana Rabbi al-'Azim' three times is sunnah; adding more does not invalidate the prayer.
There is a minority view that extra tasbih invalidates, but the relied-upon Hanafi position is that it does not.
5
Apply the consequence of omission
Omitting any sunnah action leaves the prayer valid but lacking perfection; no compensation required.
The prayer is still acceptable.

قوله (وركع ووضع يديه على ركبتيه وفرج أصابعه) لما رواه أنس من صفة صلاته عليه السلام. وأشار إلى أن التطبيق المروي عن ابن مسعود منسوخ وهو أن يضم إحدى الكفين إلى الأخرى ويرسلهما بين فخذيه بما في الصحيحين. وفي فتح القدير : ويعتمد بيديه على ركبتيه ناصباً ساقيه، وإحناؤهما شبه القوس كما يفعل عامة الناس مكروه. ذكره في روضة العلماء. وإنما يفرج بينهما لأنه أمكن من الأخذ بالركب ولا يندب إلى التفريج إلا في هذه الحالة، ولا إلى الضم إلا في حالة السجود وفيما عدا ذلك يترك على العادة. قوله (وبسط ظهره وسوى رأسه بعجزه) فإنه سنة كما صح عنه عليه السلام فلا يرفع رأسه ولا يخفضه. وفي المجتبى : والسنة في الركوع إلصاق الكعبين واستقبال الأصابع للقبلة. قوله (وسبح فيه ثلاثاً) أي في ركوعه بأن يقول سبحان ربي العظيم ثلاثاً لحديث ابن ماجه «إذا ركع أحدكم فليقل سبحان ربي العظيم ثلاثاً»، قال الحلبي : لا تفسد وقيل تفسد قال : فظاهره ترجيح عدم الفساد وعليه يتخرج صحة الشروع به، ويوافقه ما في الخلاصة معزياً إلى زلة القارىء للشهيد : لو قال الله «أكبار» يصير شارعاً . قلت : لكن ينبغي أن يقيد بما إذا لم يقصد المخالفة .اهـ. أقول : إذا كان جمعاً للكبير فلا أثر لإرادته المخالفة في اللفظ فقط . قوله : (ولعل الأكمل أراد المعنى الأول) قال في النهر : ولا يخفى أنه يجوز أن يكون فرضاً اهـ. يعني يجوز أن يكون على تنزيل مخاطب يحمله على الإقرار ثم قال في النهر بعد ذكره حاصل ما مر : وبهذا التقرير ظهر لك أن ما قاله ابن أمير حاج من أنه لا ينبغي أن يختلف في عدم صحة الشروع به مبني على أن الاستفهام حقيقي ومقتضى كونه تقريراً أن يصح قوله: (وليس هو موافقاً لما في الجامع) أي ليس موافقاً في اللفظ من حيث الإطلاق والتقييد، وليس المراد المنافاة لاحتمال أن يكون ذلك مراد الجامع إذ ليس في كلامه ما يصرفه عن ذلك. قوله : (ابن هبيرة)
His statement: 'And he performed ruku', placed his hands on his knees, and spread his fingers' due to what Anas narrated about the description of his prayer (peace be upon him). And he pointed out that the tathiq reported from Ibn Mas'ud is abrogated, which is to join one palm to the other and put them between his thighs, as in the two Sahihs. In Fath al-Qadir: 'And he leans with his hands on his knees, with his shins straight, and bending them like a bow as common people do is disliked.' Mentioned in Rawdat al-'Ulama'. He spreads them because it is more effective for grasping the knees, and spreading is not recommended except in this situation, nor is joining except in prostration; otherwise one leaves it as customary. His statement: 'And he stretched his back and made his head level with his buttocks' for it is sunnah as authentically reported from him, so he does not raise his head nor lower it. In al-Mujtaba: 'And it is sunnah in ruku' to bring the heels together and face the toes toward the qibla.' His statement: 'And he said tasbih therein three times' i.e. in his ruku', by saying 'Subhana Rabbi al-'Azim' three times due to the hadith of Ibn Majah: 'When one of you bows, let him say Subhana Rabbi al-'Azim three times.' al-Halabi said: It does not invalidate, and it is said it invalidates. He said: Its apparent is the preference of non-invalidation, and upon it is based the validity of starting with it. It is supported by what is in al-Khulasa attributed to Zillat al-Qari' by al-Shahid: If one says 'Allah Akbar' (incorrectly) he becomes a starter. I say: But it should be restricted to when he does not intend opposition. He said: I say: If it is a plural of 'kabir', then his intending opposition has no effect on the wording only. His statement: 'Perhaps al-Akmal intended the first meaning.' He said in al-Nahr: 'It is not hidden that it is permissible to be a supposition.' Meaning it is permissible to be on the basis of assuming an addressee making him acknowledge. Then he said in al-Nahr after mentioning the summary of what has passed: 'By this clarification it appears to you that what Ibn Amir Haj said – that there should be no disagreement about the invalidity of starting with it – is based on the interrogation being real, and the requirement of it being affirmation is that it is valid to start.' His statement: 'And it is not in agreement with what is in al-Jami'' meaning not in agreement in wording regarding absolute and restricted, and it is not intended as contradiction because it is possible that was the intent of al-Jami' since there is nothing in his words to turn it away from that. His statement: 'Ibn Hubayrah.'
Source pages p. 550

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the description of ruku' in prayer. It begins by explaining the sunnah of placing hands on knees and spreading fingers, citing Anas' hadith. It notes that the practice of tathiq (placing hands between thighs) is abrogated. The commentator mentions that leaning on the knees with straight shins is sunnah, while bending the back like a bow is disliked. Spreading fingers is recommended only in ruku', while joining them is recommended only in sujud. Next, it states that stretching the back and keeping the head level with the buttocks is sunnah, based on authentic hadith. It adds that it is also sunnah to bring the heels together and face toes toward qibla. Then it addresses the tasbih in ruku': saying 'Subhana Rabbi al-'Azim' three times is recommended based on the hadith of Ibn Majah. There is a discussion about whether adding more than three times invalidates the prayer, with the relied-upon view being that it does not. The passage then detours into a grammatical discussion about the takbir (from previous lesson), which seems to be an OCR interpolation. The main content concludes with the ruling that the tasbih is sunnah and the prayer remains valid.

Key Terms
التطبيق (al-tathiq): Placing one palm on the other and putting them between the thighs in ruku'; reported from Ibn Mas'ud but considered abrogated.
سبحان ربي العظيم (subhana rabbi al-'azim): The tasbih recited in ruku'; recommended to be said three times based on a hadith.
الركب (al-rukab): The knees; grasping them with hands in ruku' is part of the sunnah posture.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the ruku' posture, what is the ruling on keeping the back flat and the head level with the buttocks?
A) It is fard, and the prayer is invalid without it.
B) It is wajib, requiring sujud al-sahw if omitted.
C) It is sunnah, and the prayer is valid without it.
D) It is makruh, and should be avoided.

Key Takeaway
The sunnah actions of ruku' enhance the prayer but are not obligatory; the prayer remains valid without them.

Sunnah Acts of Ruku in Hanafi Prayer
Explaining the sunnah acts of ruku with evidence and abrogation of tatbiq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the sunnah acts of ruku in the Hanafi school and identify the hadith evidence for each.

Core Idea
The sunnah postures of ruku are based on the Prophet's practice and confirmed by hadith.

In the Hanafi school, the physical acts of bowing (ruku) are defined by specific sunnah postures that enhance the perfection of prayer. This lesson unpacks those acts, their evidence, and clarifies why tatbiq is abrogated. Understanding these details helps you align your prayer with the established sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sunnah acts listed in the text
List each act: placing hands on knees, spreading fingers, straightening back, leveling head with buttocks. Also note additional acts from al-Mujtaba: joining heels and facing toes to qibla.
These are all sunnah muakkadah.
2
Locate the dalil for each act
The primary dalil is the hadith of Anas (Bukhari and Muslim) describing the Prophet's ruku. The text also cites Fath al-Qadir and Rawdat al-'Ulama for additional details.
The hadith is ahad but accepted as evidence for sunnah.
3
Understand the abrogation of tatbiq
The text states that tatbiq (joining palms between thighs) is abrogated; the authentic practice is to place hands on knees with fingers spread. This is based on the two Sahihs.
Tatbiq was a practice of Ibn Mas'ud, but the majority hadith overrides it.
4
Analyze additional recommended postures
From Fath al-Qadir: leaning on knees with straight shins is recommended, while bending them like a bow is makruh. Also, finger spreading is only sunnah in ruku, joining in sujud.
These details come from later Hanafi commentaries.

قوله (وركع ووضع يديه على ركبتيه وفرج أصابعه لما رواه أنس من صفة صلاته عليه السلام. وأشار إلى أن التطبيق المروي عن ابن مسعود منسوخ وهو أن يضم إحدى الكفين إلى الأخرى ويرسلهما بين فخذيه بما في الصحيحين. وفي فتح القدير : ويعتمد بيديه على ركبتيه ناصباً ساقيه، وإحناؤهما شبه القوس كما يفعل عامة الناس مكروه. ذكره في روضة العلماء. وإنما يفرج بينهما لأنه أمكن من الأخذ بالركب ولا يندب إلى التفريج إلا في هذه الحالة، ولا إلى الضم إلا في حالة السجود وفيما عدا ذلك يترك على العادة. قوله (وبسط ظهره وسوى رأسه بعجزه) فإنه سنة كما صح عنه صلى الله عليه وسلم فلهذا لا يرفع رأسه ولا يخفضه. وفي المجتبى : والسنة في الركوع إلصاق الكعبين واستقبال الأصابع للقبلة.
His statement: (And he bowed and placed his hands on his knees and spread his fingers) due to what Anas narrated from the description of his prayer, peace be upon him. And he indicated that the tatbiq narrated from Ibn Mas'ud is abrogated, which is to join the two palms together and place them between his thighs, as in the two Sahihs. In Fath al-Qadir: And he leans with his hands on his knees, keeping his shins straight, and bending them like a bow as done by common people is disliked. It is mentioned in Rawdat al-'Ulama. He spreads them because it is more suitable for grasping the knees, and spreading is only recommended in this state, and joining only in prostration, and in other cases it is left to habit. His statement: (And he straightened his back and made his head level with his buttocks) for it is a sunnah as authentically reported from him, peace be upon him, so he neither raises his head nor lowers it. In al-Mujtaba: And it is sunnah in bowing to join the heels and face the toes towards the qibla.
Source pages p. 550

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof explaining the physical acts of bowing in prayer (ruku). The author cites the matn from Kanz al-Daqa'iq stating that the one praying places his hands on his knees, spreads his fingers, straightens his back, and makes his head level with his buttocks. The commentary provides the evidence from the hadith of Anas describing the Prophet's prayer. It also notes that the practice of tatbiq (joining hands between thighs) attributed to Ibn Mas'ud is abrogated according to the authentic hadiths in Bukhari and Muslim. In Fath al-Qadir, it is mentioned that leaning on the knees with straight shins is recommended, while bending them like a bow is disliked. Spreading the fingers is recommended only in ruku, while joining them is recommended in sujud. The straightening of the back and leveling the head is a sunnah, and al-Mujtaba adds that it is sunnah to join the heels and face the toes toward the qibla during bowing. All these details are based on the practice of the Prophet and are considered sunnah acts in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
ركوع (ruku): Bowing in prayer, a position where the hands are placed on the knees.
تطبيق (tatbiq): Joining the palms and placing them between the thighs during ruku.
فرج (farij): Spreading the fingers apart, specifically during ruku.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi sunnah for ruku includes specific postures with hands on knees, spread fingers, straight back, and level head, based on the hadith of Anas and confirmed by later Hanafi authorities.
